---
title: "Don't Buy Dell for Ubuntu"
date: 2009-04-23
forum: Dell Ubuntu Support (CLOSED)
---

### Post by BertP on 2009-04-23
I bought a  new Dell inspiron desktop preinstalled with Ubuntu 7,04 a little more than a year ago.  I haven't had too many problems with the hardware..  the cheap mouse did wear out after about 10 months and the power supply  died about a week after the  1 year warranty  was over but other than than I have been generally pleased with the hardware.

What I did not expect was that I would not be able to upgrade Ubuntu.  I had no problem with the online update from 7.04 to 7.10  but I found it impossible to update to 8.04.  I tried both the online update as well as the Dell iso.. When I called Dell tech support (during warranty period) all I got was a condescending Indian with a thick, hard to understand  accent telling me my only option was to revert to 7.04..... Now that 7.10  is no longer supported I will not be able to install any new applications or get any updates...  Unless the Ubuntu 9 series installs successfully (and from what I have seen on these forums, that appears to be highly doubtful)  I guess I will eventually be forced to go back to Windows ](*,)

Probably the problem is with Canonical for producing new versions that won't allow existing users to upgrade but don't fool yourself into thinking that Dell will be there for you to make Ubuntu work with their hardware during the warranty period. As the unpleasant Indian tech made clear, they could not care less!

---

### Post by anjilslaire on 2009-04-23
Try installing the vanilla Ubuntu 8.04 or the new 9.04 released today from Ubuntu.com. The Dell repositories are often outdated and unsupported fully, it seems.

Your desktop will likely be fine with regular Ubuntu. Take the Live CD for a spin and see.

### Post by BertP on 2009-04-24
I am glad all you guys have had no problems upgrading Ubuntu on your Dell computers;  Still I think it is important to let the prospective buyer know that:

 1) Just because Dell preloaded your system with Ubuntu does not mean that the computer will be compatible with the next major release of Ubuntu.

2) And if you have trouble installing  even a Dell specific release of Ubuntu during your warranty period,  Dell tech support is unwilling to help

### Post by snowpine on 2009-04-24
If you are new to Linux, you need to educate yourself on how the different distros work. 

Ubuntu is designed to be updated every 6 months or so. If you want to keep the same release of Ubuntu on your computer for more than 18 months, you need to use a long term support release (like 8.04 Hardy Heron) which is supported for 3 years.

If you are the type of user who does not like upgrading to new releases, Ubuntu is a bad choice for you. I highly recommend a "rolling release" distro like Arch or Debian testing (if you like getting new versions of applications) or Debian Lenny (if you want something really stable and unchanging).

---

If you are the type of user who does not like upgrading to new releases, Ubuntu is a bad choice for you. I highly recommend a "rolling release" distro like Arch or Debian testing (if you like getting new versions of applications) or Debian Lenny (if you want something really stable and unchanging).

### Post by skillllllz on 2009-04-24
> **BertP said:**
> I don't mind updating my OS.... as long as those updates WORK!

Exactly what is the issue? Where do you run into a problem and what messages, if any, are you seeing on your machine?

*Edit*
Just went back and looked at your first post. DO NOT ever use a Dell ISO to attempt an in-place upgrade. What you need to do is download and burn the 8.04 "Alternate ISO." Then insert the disc into your already running 7.10 system. You will be prompted to upgrade. Follow the prompts and it will do the upgrade.

---

### Post by gbrainin on 2009-04-24
The switch from 7.10 to 8.04 is when the IDE/RAID problem showed up in the 530s.  This is a known problem with an easy workaround published by Dell: see [http://http://linux.dell.com/wiki/index.php/Ubuntu_8.04/Issues/Desktop_Wont_Boot]("http://http://linux.dell.com/wiki/index.php/Ubuntu_8.04/Issues/Desktop_Wont_Boot").

Assuming you called Dell's regular tech support line, I'm surprised they even knew enough about Linux to suggest downgrading.  If you called the dedicated Linux support line (866-622-1947), then I'm sorely disappointed in the degradation of their service; they were quite good last time I called them.

Given the IDE/RAID issue, above, chances are you should be able to upgrade, either using the upgrade manager or using any standard Ubuntu CD (live or alternate).  I've used both methods successfully on 530s.  If that doesn't work, a more specific description of what goes wrong may help us diagnose your unique problem.

---

### Post by gbrainin on 2009-04-24
> **BertP said:**
> I believe after each attempted install I got initramfs error messages displayed to the terminal  by BusyBoy.

Yup, that's what the IDE/RAID problem does.  Follow the instructions on the Dell Linux Wiki, and I bet it'll work.  I would suggest first trying to simply load the 8.XX live CD with the RAID mode turned on; if that works, you're probably good to go.

### Post by skillllllz on 2009-04-24
> **BertP said:**
> Thanks for the help everyone!


I will not be in a  position to make the change to my computer until tonight but can you tell me what are the implications of putting it into RAID mode?  I didn't receive a  motherboard manual and am somewhat embarrassed to admit that I don't know anything at all about the motherboard.

Can someone point me to documentation about the RAID capabilities of the 530N's motherboard?  As inexpensive as hard drives have become obtaining a real RAID system  is an appealing thought

The 530 does not actually have a RAID controller. The RAID option enables/disables [AHCI]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Host_Controller_Interface") for the SATA drive.

---

### Post by gbrainin on 2009-04-24
AFAICT, the only downside of switching to RAID mode is that various flavors of Windows won't work with it.  So if you're dual-booting, you have to use the other workaround.

I have never been able to get a really authoritative explanation of the IDE/RAID switch in the BIOS, but as skillllllz said, it's not actually RAID, more like allows for software-RAID to be used.  I've been using it for over a year now, and have perceived no difference.

---

### Post by skillllllz on 2009-04-24
> **gbrainin said:**
> AFAICT, the only downside of switching to RAID mode is that various flavors of Windows won't work with it.  So if you're dual-booting, you have to use the other workaround.

I have never been able to get a really authoritative explanation of the IDE/RAID switch in the BIOS, but as skillllllz said, it's not actually RAID, more like allows for software-RAID to be used.  I've been using it for over a year now, and have perceived no difference.

Windows Vista and 7 can utilize AHCI right out of the box; XP requires special drivers to be installed which, for some folks, can sometimes be a nightmare.

---

### Post by skillllllz on 2009-04-24
> **BertP said:**
> I would definitely prefer an ISO that would allow me  keep my current applications,  data and drivers instead of totally wiping my drive! 


 by  "alternate ISO" do you mean  the alternate non-graphical ISO of 8.04 available from Ubuntu.com?   I know the Dell ISO will wipe my drive, will the regular Ubuntu.com "Live CD"  wipe my drive or not allow me to choose video drivers during install?

Thanks again for the help!

Yes, the alternate non-graphical ISO, available from ubuntu.com, allows you to upgrade your previous version of Ubuntu without losing anything. Just boot into your PC as you normally do and insert the CD, it will prompt you to upgrade. This is called the Off-line upgrade.

---

### Post by gbrainin on 2009-04-24
> **BertP said:**
> I would definitely prefer an ISO that would allow me  keep my current applications,  data and drivers instead of totally wiping my drive!

For future reference (after you get over this hurdle), you may want to consider creating a separate partition for your /home directory.  That's where all your data, and the preferences for most programs, are kept.  Reinstalling the programs, IMO, is not that big a deal (Applications->Add/Remove); I just keep a list on a piece of paper.  But that's a matter of personal preference.

Instructions for creating a separate /home partition are here: [http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/separatehome]("http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/separatehome").  Be sure you understand all the commands and concepts before you start, though; you don't want to get lost halfway through.

---

### Post by gbrainin on 2009-04-25
I'm no expert on reading logfiles, but I always try to narrow down the possible sources of trouble first.  I'd go back to my original suggestion: Get yourself a live CD and try to boot from that.  If it works, at least you know the problem isn't hardware incompatibility.

Also if it works, you may want to consider a fresh install instead of continuing to bash your head against the upgrade wall.  Yes, theoretically it's a less elegant solution (the upgrade _ought_ to work), but it'll probably take less time and effort, even including the time you'll have to spend backing up and/or putting /home on a separate partition.

---

### Post by wyth on 2009-04-25
> **BertP said:**
> 1) Just because Dell preloaded your system with Ubuntu does not mean that the computer will be compatible with the next major release of Ubuntu.

That's not necessarily a Dell problem -- *ANY* computer may face upgrade issues depending on changes in the kernel, developments with software and how it interacts with older software, etc.  Just check any thread of any forum for any operating system -- Windows and Mac included; your Nvidia card or Intel wireless may have worked wonderfully when you firs got your (name your hardware and OS here) machine, but that never guarantees that your hardware will always work the same for ever and ever.

> **BertP said:**
> 2) And if you have trouble installing  even a Dell specific release of Ubuntu during your warranty period,  Dell tech support is unwilling to help 

I'll just say that there s a Linux-specific number you need to call, and every time I've called it, I've had great help.  But if you don't get the right number, the people you reach may not know what you're really talking about.  (The number is on the sticky for the Dell thread here.)

But it's also my experience that the more info a user can bring to the table -- whether to the help desk at Dell or to a forum -- the more effective help that user receives.  What didn't work?  How?  Why?  There's a logical chain to follow, but you need a few starting points.

I bought a Dell Inspiron 530 with 7.04 on it a few years back.  The only issue I had was when the city power went on and off a bunch of times during a storm and zapped my motherboard.  Dell sent someone to replace it in two days (it was under warranty).  

Other than that, I've had no problems.  However, I went up to 8.04 with a downloaded iso from Ubuntu, not Dell.

### Post by kkkkdddd on 2009-04-29
> **BertP said:**
> 
File "/tmp/tmp.NRmgZB7180/hardy", line 36, in <module>
    filemode='w')
  File "/tmp/tmp.NRmgZB7180/__init__.py", line 1240, in basicConfig
    
  File "/tmp/tmp.NRmgZB7180/__init__.py", line 770, in __init__
    
IOError: [Errno 13] Permission denied: '/var/log/dist-upgrade/main.log'


Maybe you have to set permission for main.log.
```
sudo chmod 666 /var/log/dist-upgrade/main.log
```

And close this file in all editors.

### Post by recluce on 2009-05-01
> **BertP said:**
> I am glad all you guys have had no problems upgrading Ubuntu on your Dell computers;  Still I think it is important to let the prospective buyer know that:

 1) Just because Dell preloaded your system with Ubuntu does not mean that the computer will be compatible with the next major release of Ubuntu.

The lesson is a different one, I believe. NEVER use any OS preinstalled by Dell or any other OEM. Be it Windows XP, Vista, Ubuntu or whatever. I find that these installation allways underperform, have errors, omissions, incompatibilities, outdated software and useless bloatware.

I would suggest to the prospective buyer: be sure to have a Dell recovery CD/DVD to reset the system to factory specifications, if ever needed for any reason - and than format the drive and do a fresh install.

At least this has been my personal experience with the following Dell laptops: Inspiron 7000,7500,8000,8100,8200, Latitude D800, D810, D820, D830, D610, D630 and Vostro 1000.  

> 
2) And if you have trouble installing  even a Dell specific release of Ubuntu during your warranty period,  Dell tech support is unwilling to help

Either you have booked and paid for Business support (in which case you should not end up in India, when calling from North America) or you should consider yourself without support - just like it is with Microsoft.

In other words: regular support is useless, if you believe that you need software/OS support, buy the business support option.

---

### Post by recluce on 2009-05-01
> **skillllllz said:**
> Windows Vista and 7 can utilize AHCI right out of the box; XP requires special drivers to be installed which, for some folks, can sometimes be a nightmare.

Just one more example why you should always fresh-install a new system with OEM-installed OS. The last Dell D630 and D830 we got came with Windows XP preinstalled. Instead of installing the Intel SATA drivers (which are posted on the Dell support site), Dell just turned the BIOS to "IDE emulation". 

How lame is that?

For the end user, it makes a reinstall of XP mandatory, as I know of no other way than to slipstream the Intel drivers into the XP install CD (unless you still have a floppy drive).

I am so happy that Ubuntu doesn't require rigging like that...

---

### Post by superm1 on 2009-05-01
> **recluce said:**
> The lesson is a different one, I believe. NEVER use any OS preinstalled by Dell or any other OEM. Be it Windows XP, Vista, Ubuntu or whatever. I find that these installation allways underperform, have errors, omissions, incompatibilities, outdated software and useless bloatware.

I can assure you this is not the case for *every* *OEM* installation.  A few specific examples:

**Mini 9**
The mini 9 image will boot far faster than 8.04 or 8.10 would have booted at the time it was produced.  The only "bloatware" you'll get with it is a set of Yahoo icons in the mini9 dell-bar and the Yahoo toolbar pre-installed.  Otherwise it's a very slim installation.
Omissions?  What's missing?
Outdated software?  There's a fine line between a stable release and outdated software, especially with open source software.  Take a look how 8.10 was when it launched.  It's not even funny how many instabilities were introduced.  You couldn't even change the brightness without locking up X on most Dell laptops.

**Other Dell Ubuntu installs**
There is absolutely nothing that can be considered bloatware in a Dell factory install for any other Ubuntu preload.  The only things that are "added" on top of the normal Ubuntu DVD livefs are a recovery media creation suite, codecs, dvd support, and extra drivers.  Where in this is the "bloatware" you clame?
Yes, the factory install is larger that a normal Ubuntu CD install, but that is because it contains all of the language support.  It's the same thing you get if you do an Ubuntu DVD install with all languages selected.

---

### Post by BertP on 2009-05-01
For anyone following this, I finally did successfully upgrade to 8.04 LTS.

 All it took was changing my bios to RAID mode, a full backup of my user directory,  burning and installing from a Desktop Edition CD created from a blank newer than 5 years (I found out that the blanks apparently degrade too) and then restoring my user directory.

It was actually much easier than I thought.  Ubuntu even provided a "protected driver notification" which gave me the option of installing the Nvidia protected driver.  So if you know how to install which gstreamer codecs you need, and can install the flash plugin for firefox, you can  have all the capabilities of the DELL preinstalled Ubuntu version working  for your fresh "Ubuntu Desktop Edition" within minutes of installation.

 (I think someone in this thread suggested that I create a separate partition for my home directory. I did take that advice and hopefully it will make upgrading easier in the future)

Thanks again to everyone that helped me through this!

---

### Post by uconvert on 2009-07-25
I am sorry about your negative experience with DELL/Ubuntu. I discovered Ubuntu back in the days of Dapper, and have used it on every computer I have owned since, dual booting with XP on a few. 

In 2007, I built my own barebone laptop (ASUS Z62FM) exclusively for Ubuntu. Everything worked except suspend to ram - a common complaint among laptop users.

In 2008, my GF bought a Dell Inspiron 1525 with (made for?) MS Vista Home Premium for her university studies - needing MS Office 2007. Everyday for a year she has cursed Vista while I have smiled using my ASUS with Ubuntu.

Then the Jaunty Jackalope arrived, and I began cursing Jaunty because of the Intel Video problem and Adobe Flashplayer, but I knew the community would be hard at work with workarounds and they have come through brilliantly.

Last week, my GF slammed her Dell shut and started to throw it out the door, so I intervened and told her I would load my ASUS with XP for her, she could boot into XP on our Desktop and I would take the DELL 1525.

What a great deal for me - she is now a happy PC user and I am an ecstatic Ubuntified PC user on a revived one year old Dell :D

I downloaded the lastest 9.04 Alternate Install, set up my quirky partition requirements (root, home, vbox [40Gb VirtualBox for XP software], a small fat32 for one old windows app run with Codeweaver Crossover Pro), and the only glitch was the another Intel X3100 video workaround; but that was an easy fix. Now Compiz is working flawlessly and for the first time in several years, I have a laptop that will suspend to ram, wakeup and reconnect to the internet. I removed the Vista sticker from my palmrest and ordered a sticker for the palmrest and a sticker for the super key from system76.

For most issues, its usually just a hardware conflict that has already been resolved by somebody, just keep looking through the forums.

Good luck

### Post by zkriesse on 2009-07-29
Got a Dell Dimension E521 and the Ubuntu 9.04 Server and Desktop won't work on it...get to install point then it tells me that the disk couldn't be mounted.....any ya'll know what that's 'bout?

---

### Post by Crunchy the Headcrab on 2009-07-29
> **Zachk18 said:**
> Got a Dell Dimension E521 and the Ubuntu 9.04 Server and Desktop won't work on it...get to install point then it tells me that the disk couldn't be mounted.....any ya'll know what that's 'bout?

You might need to change your disk controller settings in your bios.  On my Asus the options are: enhanced sata, raid, and compatibility.  I have to run in compatibility for Linux to work.  Unfortunately there is a system corrupting bug in Ubuntu (when running compatibility mode) for my particular controller, so I can't use Ubuntu 9.04.  Currently using Fedora 11.  You can try Ubuntu, and if you get a corrupted file system then switch to a different version or different distro.

### Post by RedRat on 2009-12-31
Well I bought a Dell 530n with Ubuntu 7.04 installed. I screwed the machine up by trying to install Ubuntu Studio and basically had to re-install Ubuntu from scratch. Dell sent a disk with the machine, that I found out later, was merely Canonical Ubuntu 7.04 vanilla. I completely reformated the hard drive and installed from that Canonical Disk. No problems with the install. I upgraded via synaptic to 7.10 and then to 8.04.

Yes, I did have problems with the video card drivers in moving between upgrades, the worst being from 7.10 to 8.04. In point of fact, every kernel upgrade in the first year of 8.04 was a pain. But I note now that these seemed to have disappeared. Keep in mind that his straight vanilla Ubuntu, not the Dell version. 

My machine has been running steadily since I bought it in December of 2007. No problems with the mouse, keyboard, or power supply. I must say, I am impressed.

All that being said, I wonder what will happen when I upgrade to 10.04. Basically, I am satisfied with staying with the LTS versions Ubuntu, I don't believe in being on the bleeding edge of OSs, you just get bloodied.
